<div id="review_3356063095" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3356063095" />
    <a title="YouKneeK" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek"><img alt="YouKneeK" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386369592p2/25923230.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3356063095?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="YouKneeK" class="user" itemprop="url" name="YouKneeK" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek">YouKneeK</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/39970390-parable-of-the-talents">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=completed-series">completed-series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3356063095" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15361840892636505768"><i>Parable of the Talents</i> is the second and final book in Octavia E. Butler’s <i>Earthseed</i> duology. I have learned this was initially intended to be a longer series. Each of the two published books end with relatively satisfying conclusions though, providing a sense of both closure and of more possibilities ahead. It didn’t feel unfinished, although I do think I would have enjoyed the direction further books would have taken.<br/><br/>I don’t think I can say what this book is about without spoiling the first on</span>
  <span id="freeText15361840892636505768" style="display:none"><i>Parable of the Talents</i> is the second and final book in Octavia E. Butler’s <i>Earthseed</i> duology. I have learned this was initially intended to be a longer series. Each of the two published books end with relatively satisfying conclusions though, providing a sense of both closure and of more possibilities ahead. It didn’t feel unfinished, although I do think I would have enjoyed the direction further books would have taken.<br /><br />I don’t think I can say what this book is about without spoiling the first one, so I’m going to put that in spoiler tags. I tried not to go into enough detail to spoil this book. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The group that decided to settle on Bankole’s land at the end of the previous book makes a home for themselves there and their group continues to grow. Meanwhile, a growing religious faction that refers to itself as “Christian America” takes extreme measures against people who don’t share their beliefs, ranging from petty criminals to annoying homeless people, and most especially cults. Of course, as we saw in the previous book, Lauren is starting a fledgling cult of her own, so you can imagine where the story might go from there. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I liked the first book better than this one, mainly because the story went in a direction I found less interesting. I think Butler was a great writer. Her writing consistently draws me into her stories quickly, holds my attention, and has depth and usually gives me things to think about. She has a way of writing challenging sorts of characters who I care about but whose choices I don’t always agree with. The main problem I had with the story was that it continues in a big way with one of the themes I particularly didn’t care for in the first book. So it’s more clear now where the author was going with that, but it led to a lot of content that I tend not to enjoy very much in stories in general and which I occasionally got tired of in this book. Despite that, there were still several aspects of the story I found more interesting and my desire to find out what would happen to the characters helped hold my interest.<br /><br />I’m rating this at 3.5 stars but rounding down to 3 on Goodreads.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15361840892636505768" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_98921664" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/98921664" />
    <a title="Jamie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/404400-jamie"><img alt="Jamie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520026973p2/404400.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/98921664?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jamie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie" href="/user/show/404400-jamie">Jamie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer98921664" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12306036471477052350">I loved the first book, but could hardly bear to finish this one. The first half is really boring, and then there's a brief but extremely horrible and violent section, where evil, white Christian men rape, torture, and murder people who don't agree with their views. It's way over the top. Then it's boring again until the end.<br/><br/>Part of the boredom stems from the way this book is written. Unlike <i>Parable of the Sower</i>, which steeps the reader in the middle of the drama, this book consists entirely of </span>
  <span id="freeText12306036471477052350" style="display:none">I loved the first book, but could hardly bear to finish this one. The first half is really boring, and then there's a brief but extremely horrible and violent section, where evil, white Christian men rape, torture, and murder people who don't agree with their views. It's way over the top. Then it's boring again until the end.<br /><br />Part of the boredom stems from the way this book is written. Unlike <i>Parable of the Sower</i>, which steeps the reader in the middle of the drama, this book consists entirely of diary entries that continue the first story (but told in fragments missing big chunks of time), and bold texted narrative written by Olamina's daughter. The religious side get tedious and a bit lecturing. I imagine L. Ron Hubbard at his desk trying to think of ways to start writing his Scientology when Olamina (who is pretty much nameless in this book -- the journals being in first-person and all) recites her verses over and over again. I also think it is a huge jump to go from poor, desperate woman to someone who can seduce anyone with her words, which is how she ends up within the last 50 pages or so, when her cult begins to grow. <br /><br />I enjoyed the parts about her daughter's life the most. Though brief, there are some tantalizing descriptions of future technology and a society that, after a bad glitch, auto-corrects itself, are interesting. <br /><br />In any case, I think the first book was far better by itself than with this as a sequel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12306036471477052350" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_172926537" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/172926537" />
    <a title="Sean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3870575-sean"><img alt="Sean" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578300713p2/3870575.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/172926537?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean" href="/user/show/3870575-sean">Sean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780446675789" href="/book/show/764646.Parable_of_the_Talents">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3870575-sean?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3870575-sean?shelf=life-philosophy">life-philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3870575-sean?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer172926537" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16256766487554685351">I don't feel capable of adequately putting down my thoughts on this book quite yet. But I'll write some stuff. Parable of the Talents and Sower before it are both grand accomplishments in inspiring deep self reflective thought while also entertaining the reader with deep and relatable characters. For many years now I have been struggling with how I should determine my attitude toward religion and belief. Though my inquiry into understanding the true nature of faith and religion is far from over,</span>
  <span id="freeText16256766487554685351" style="display:none">I don't feel capable of adequately putting down my thoughts on this book quite yet. But I'll write some stuff. Parable of the Talents and Sower before it are both grand accomplishments in inspiring deep self reflective thought while also entertaining the reader with deep and relatable characters. For many years now I have been struggling with how I should determine my attitude toward religion and belief. Though my inquiry into understanding the true nature of faith and religion is far from over, Butler has been able to show me some insightful avenues of thought, but perhaps also stoked the fires of fear that lurk under my misunderstanding. If not for that fear, I would not feel any inclination to investigate at all. I am taken aback by it; what do I have to fear and thereby hate within the cathartic belief systems of others? Lack of understanding breeds fear, which leads to bitterness, hate and judgement. I like to understand people and processes, if anything that would be my purpose in life so far. Through understanding the belief system created by Butler throughout these novels, Earthseed, I have also gained insight into some of the characteristics of religion in general. <br /><br />The protagonist of the Sower novels, Lauren Olamina, is a character for whom I have great respect. She is a realist, Earthseed is largely based on the fundamental concept that the world is what we make of it and yet we are subject to it even then. Change happens but we are not powerless to shape that change. Through her condition as a "sharer", a brain disorder which causes her sympathetic reaction to manifest physically and uncontrollably, Olamina is a master of understanding the feelings of others. Through this understanding she is capable of bringing people together, converting them to her religion. However I still feel that she is blind in subtle ways. Her steadfast faith that her beliefs are unequivocal truths makes her vulnerable, while she created Earthseed largely to help people understand this post-apocalyptic world, survive in it and become stronger, a contradiction. She doesn't seem to understand why some people, such as her husband, brother and eventually daughter, just don't believe the way she does. Much in her character reminds me of Orson Scott Card's Ender Wiggins (Speaker for the Dead). Ender had an uncanny way of understanding people and the situations that surround them. But his faith, if it could be said that he had one, was simply that of understanding. Ender could show people all of the illusions that had mislead them into hate and fear, but he wouldn't create illusions even to give hope and purpose. Earthseed may paint a harsh and mundane picture of existence, but at least it promised purpose and Destiny. These two characters seem to personify two sides of the narrowing range of ideas that I am searching through for understanding of the world I live in.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16256766487554685351" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2661538484" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2661538484" />
    <a title="Dave" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35045983-dave"><img alt="Dave" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1496413985p2/35045983.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2661538484?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dave" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dave" href="/user/show/35045983-dave">Dave</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/39970390-parable-of-the-talents">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35045983-dave?shelf=post-apocalyptic-fiction">post-apocalyptic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35045983-dave?shelf=read-borrowed">read-borrowed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35045983-dave?shelf=science-fiction-not-classic">science-fiction-not-classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35045983-dave?shelf=science-fiction-all">science-fiction-all</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2661538484" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer616710803580053029"><strong>Nasty, Brutish, and Short</strong><br/><br/>Thomas Hobbes explained that without a society of rules, laws, customs, there would be: <br/><br/>"No arts; no letters; no society; and which is worst of all, continual fear, and danger of violent death: and the life of man, solitary, poor, nasty, brutish and short."<br/><br/>Octavia Butler's two Earthseed novels, like many a post-apocalypse or dystopian novel, imagines a world where society has broken down and all concepts of right and wrong, justice or injustice have all but disappeared. </span>
  <span id="freeText616710803580053029" style="display:none"><strong>Nasty, Brutish, and Short</strong><br /><br />Thomas Hobbes explained that without a society of rules, laws, customs, there would be: <br /><br />"No arts; no letters; no society; and which is worst of all, continual fear, and danger of violent death: and the life of man, solitary, poor, nasty, brutish and short."<br /><br />Octavia Butler's two Earthseed novels, like many a post-apocalypse or dystopian novel, imagines a world where society has broken down and all concepts of right and wrong, justice or injustice have all but disappeared. Her concept is that it doesn't have to be that way if we meet our full potential by spreading our seeds to the Stars. A third Earthseed novel was in the works for many years but never completed when Butler met her untimely death at 58. That novel may have led the charge to explore new worlds, leaving this broken one behind. <br /><br />In the first novel in this duology ( a trilogy minus one), Butler pictured a world where there wasn't one great cataclysmic event, but where things just fell apart and, for those on the bottom, there was nothing left but the pack on your back as the drug addicts burned and looted the surviving neighborhoods. Out on the highways, it was bare survival at best. That novel was carried by the sheer force of the narrative voice - honest, open, forthright. <br /><br />The second novel has the Earthseed community - a sort of commune in the Northern California hills - thriving. But the world around is still going to hell and that he'll descends on Acorn, this new community. Not as much of a quest here, but a realization that perhaps it's too late. The narrative voice here wasn't quite as strong, but perhaps this was meant to be more of a transitional novel to the third and perhaps other novels. <br /><br />Like the first novel, it is a brutal world where those who are weak are enslaved with electronic collars and torn asunder from each other in reeducation camps. However, it was probably not the best choice to make fundamentalist Christians as the evil slaver rapist protagonists. That detracted from the authenticity of the narrative voice. <br /><br />The new Earthseed religion or philosophy doesn't necessarily play a huge role, except to unify the themes of birth, growth, change, and regeneration. The narrator -Lauren - ultimately explains that space colonization is so expensive, so fraught with risk, that only a religious conviction could propel it along. <br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="616710803580053029" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2115236336" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2115236336" />
    <a title="Megan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10303433-megan"><img alt="Megan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506476466p2/10303433.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2115236336?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Megan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Megan" href="/user/show/10303433-megan">Megan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781888363814" href="/book/show/1345816.Parable_of_the_Talents">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10303433-megan?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10303433-megan?shelf=5-star">5-star</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2115236336" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer231719724211886704">4.5, bumped to the 5 side after reflection<br/><br/>---<br/><br/>Without a doubt one of the most comprehensive and successful sci-fi/dystopian novels I've ever read - and that's saying something, considering how dystopia has proliferated the markets for the past 5-10 years. It didn't get a full five stars because, 1) it is incredibly difficult to read because the subject matter is so, so grim; 2) I just could not reconcile the irrational and dedicated disgust Larkin/Asha harbored for her mother even after she read </span>
  <span id="freeText231719724211886704" style="display:none">4.5, bumped to the 5 side after reflection<br /><br />---<br /><br />Without a doubt one of the most comprehensive and successful sci-fi/dystopian novels I've ever read - and that's saying something, considering how dystopia has proliferated the markets for the past 5-10 years. It didn't get a full five stars because, 1) it is incredibly difficult to read because the subject matter is so, so grim; 2) I just could not reconcile the irrational and dedicated disgust Larkin/Asha harbored for her mother even after she read Lauren's memoirs and knowing everything that happened to her. Nobody says a reaction like that has to be logical, but I just didn't enjoy that facet of the novel and thought it felt contrived.<br /><br />But as for what this book did right, it's difficult to know where to begin. Maybe with this:<br /><br />In its published year of 1998, this book would - and I believe, did - stand out for its originality, its prescience, and its raw honesty. Reading it in 2017, with white nationalist violence surging in the US and the collapse of our education and health care systems looming, and with a <i>certain vile president with too many resemblances to Andrew Steele Jarrett for any degree of comfort,</i> is an entirely different sort of experience. There were moments when I was legitimately chilled and deeply disturbed by this text. <br /><br />Ultimately, this book succeeds where the majority of other dystopian novels fail because it isn't afraid to <b>go there.</b> The critical failing of current dystopian novels, particularly within YA, stems from the seeming inability these (majority white) authors have to identify the <b>problems</b> and <b>perpetrators</b> of current issues. Or else, they focus on one (oftentimes white feminist) issue and take it to its extremes. Nothing objectively wrong with that, perhaps, if it wasn't a trend. <br /><br />But Butler's <i>Talents</i> series creates a society and a world that feels natural - if horrible - because she doesn't shy away from calling out <i>multiple</i> sources of violence. Her world is one where environmental destruction has wreaked serious consequences and drastically changed the landscape of our country, resulting in mass relocation efforts and widespread poverty. It's a world where public education has collapsed because selfish people no longer feel like paying taxes to educate children, and the resulting illiteracy and vagrancy causes a massively destructive drug epidemic and - again- widespread poverty. It's a world where our dedication to nationalism and the oppression of women and minorities leads to the election of a demagogue whose followers - with his tacit approval - begin massacring and enslaving the poor, the vulnerable, and the disobedient. It's a world where corporations have begun placing their poor workers into indentured servitude even as they claim to be providing them with a livelihood. It's a world where the rich wall themselves off from the society their greed and callousness created. It's a world where people of many ethnicities live alongside one another but one where this is still, sometimes, an issue. <br /><br />It's our world, if we don't start admitting certain truths and - as Earthseed preaches - begin to act with purpose.</span>
  <a data-text-id="231719724211886704" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer155186149" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7283159999020464642">This book is the sequel to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/154578593' rel='nofollow noopener'>Parable of the Sower</a>, but it stands up pretty well by itself, though I would definitely recommend reading the first book, because Butler is that good and these books are very powerful. In <i>Parable of the Talents</i>, Lauren Olamina, the protagonist of the first book, continues trying to build a community and a following devoted to her new religion, "Earthseed." Unfortunately, she is trying to found this new religion just when America, in the grip of a near-apocalyptic econo</span>
  <span id="freeText7283159999020464642" style="display:none">This book is the sequel to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/154578593" rel="nofollow noopener">Parable of the Sower</a>, but it stands up pretty well by itself, though I would definitely recommend reading the first book, because Butler is that good and these books are very powerful. In <i>Parable of the Talents</i>, Lauren Olamina, the protagonist of the first book, continues trying to build a community and a following devoted to her new religion, "Earthseed." Unfortunately, she is trying to found this new religion just when America, in the grip of a near-apocalyptic economic and environmental collapse, elects a witch-burning fundie Talibaptist for President. Lauren and her people are literally enslaved, and Lauren's infant daughter is taken away from her.<br /><br />This is a dark book, a truly horrific dystopia, but the rape and violence does not read like a gratuitous admixture the way it does in so many books. You know how some authors want to make their books <b>extra dark</b> to let you know that these are Very Bad Times and Very Bad People, so they toss in a little rape, a little dismemberment, like one of those buckets o' blood horror films that just wind up being too schlocky and over-the-top to really horrify you? Octavia Butler doesn't do that. Instead, Lauren tells us what happened to her and her people in very clear but non-graphic terms, and the impact is felt for the rest of the story because even though she is trying to start a hopeful new religion, she <b>hates</b> her abusers with the heat of a thousand burning suns and makes no bones about it. It's very refreshing. None of that "I have to get past this" or forgiveness bullshit. She does survive and eventually launch her movement, successfully, but it's not like "Oh, and along the way some bad stuff happened."<br /><br />More horrific is the fact that Butler wrote this in 1998, and while the raving crush-the-poor blame-everything-on-brown-people sentiment was certainly alive then, Butler probably meant to exaggerate things a bit to make the country seem so horribly out of control. Today, while we're not exactly in the throes of Butler's "pox," the sentiments of President Steel could come right out of the mouths of some of the current crop of GOP candidates.<br /><br /><i>Parable of the Talents</i> is also, indirectly, a mother-daughter story. It's told in the past tense through the journals of both Lauren Olamina and her daughter, whom she never knew until her daughter reached adulthood. Her daughter has a very difficult time coming to terms with who her mother was, and so there are two very different narrative threads woven through the events described in the novel: Lauren, describing much of it as it was happening, and her daughter, commenting (and often, passing judgment) decades later.<br /><br />This is one of those science fiction books that really should be considered literature, and it's a shame Octavia Butler isn't more widely known. It's even more of a shame that she died before she could write the third book she planned. I give both of the <i>Earthseed</i> books a very high recommendation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7283159999020464642" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/225325-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/225325-jessica?shelf=sf">sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/225325-jessica?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>,

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9607048" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14414792116886839356">God, I was so into this - even more than Parable of the Sower. I've never experienced a narrator like this - a young black woman founding a new religion in a post apocalyptic world. In this book, she's up against the Christian America movement, whose leader is elected president and whose Crusaders are given a free hand to destroy or enslave "heathens" and other undesirables. Some of the early rhetoric of the Christian America movement was eerily reminiscent of that which surrounded George W. Bus</span>
  <span id="freeText14414792116886839356" style="display:none">God, I was so into this - even more than Parable of the Sower. I've never experienced a narrator like this - a young black woman founding a new religion in a post apocalyptic world. In this book, she's up against the Christian America movement, whose leader is elected president and whose Crusaders are given a free hand to destroy or enslave "heathens" and other undesirables. Some of the early rhetoric of the Christian America movement was eerily reminiscent of that which surrounded George W. Bush's presidency, which makes the fact that Butler wrote this before his term so amazing to me.<br /><br />It's a tough read at times, emotionally - the chaotic world they're surviving breeds lots of slavery and rape and murder. But I couldn't put it down. I love this writer so much. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14414792116886839356" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Ana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1991298-ana"><img alt="Ana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528559847p2/1991298.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3452195976?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ana" href="/user/show/1991298-ana">Ana</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

            Shelves:

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3452195976" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10433269301123037284"><b>Talk about foreshadowing future trouble...</b><br/><br/>The religious impulse, the need to name this newly made religion and preach it, the shortsightedness of it all... I can see why Butler uses the Biblical allegory to overthrow Judeo-Christian values and the patriarchy, but I could not relate with Olamina at all and I couldn’t care for her made-up beliefs. As a novel of self-awakening and self-fulfilment, however, it was absolutely fascinating.</span>

<div id="review_1726748862" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1726748862" />
    <a title="Margaret" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5629372-margaret"><img alt="Margaret" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339731862p2/5629372.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1726748862?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Margaret" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Margaret" href="/user/show/5629372-margaret">Margaret</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5629372-margaret?shelf=fairytale-myth-inspired">fairytale-myth-inspired</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5629372-margaret?shelf=apocalypse-dystopia">apocalypse-dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5629372-margaret?shelf=aoc">aoc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5629372-margaret?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1726748862" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17843397891353083215">Parable of the Talents begins about a decade after <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52397.Parable_of_the_Sower__Earthseed___1_' title='Parable of the Sower (Earthseed, #1) by Octavia E. Butler' rel='nofollow noopener'>Parable of the Sower</a>. Acorn is now a thriving community of 60+ members, and Lauren continues writing in her journal. Interspersed between her journal entries are the thoughts of her daughter--Asha. Though Asha is yet to be born during Lauren's sections, you immediately know that the future Asha is separated from her mother at some point. Parable of the Talents tells the story of how mother and daughter were separated, and how Acorn collapsed.<br/><br/>I l</span>
  <span id="freeText17843397891353083215" style="display:none">Parable of the Talents begins about a decade after <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52397.Parable_of_the_Sower__Earthseed___1_" title="Parable of the Sower (Earthseed, #1) by Octavia E. Butler" rel="nofollow noopener">Parable of the Sower</a>. Acorn is now a thriving community of 60+ members, and Lauren continues writing in her journal. Interspersed between her journal entries are the thoughts of her daughter--Asha. Though Asha is yet to be born during Lauren's sections, you immediately know that the future Asha is separated from her mother at some point. Parable of the Talents tells the story of how mother and daughter were separated, and how Acorn collapsed.<br /><br />I loved Parable of the Sowers so much, I had no doubt that I would enjoy this one. And then, well, I didn't. First, I found Asha's entries to be vapid. Her critical thinking skills are practically nonexistent. And then, there's the rape. A LOT of rape, I mean, every single guy but one rapes or sexually abuses women at some point, and that one exception is a manipulating asshole. This novel is a barrage of every possible abuse against women, and it's too much for me. I can handle violence, and violence against women, certainly Parable of the Sowers had that, but this was excessive. <br /><br />I did find the politics disturbing, given the upcoming election. Jarret has some frightening parallels to Trump. Here's one of the Parables of Earthseed, from this novel, as advice for voters. Something we all need to remember:<br /><br />Choose your leaders<br />with wisdom and<br />forethought.<br />To be led by a coward<br />is to be controlled<br />by all that coward fears.<br />To be led by a fool <br />is to be led<br />by the opportunists<br />who control the fool.<br />To be led by a thief<br />is to offer up<br />your most precious treasures<br />to be stolen.<br />To be led by a liar<br />is to ask<br />to be told lies.<br />To be led by a tyrant<br />is to sell yourself<br />and those you love<br />into slavery.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17843397891353083215" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3199916070" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3199916070" />
    <a title="Dustin Crazy little brown owl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2454496-dustin-crazy-little-brown-owl"><img alt="Dustin Crazy little brown owl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1320959271p2/2454496.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3199916070?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dustin Crazy little brown owl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dustin Crazy little brown owl" href="/user/show/2454496-dustin-crazy-little-brown-owl">Dustin Crazy little brown owl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781538732199" href="/book/show/41161350-parable-of-the-talents">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2454496-dustin-crazy-little-brown-owl?shelf=controversial">controversial</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2454496-dustin-crazy-little-brown-owl?shelf=religion-or-lack-thereof">religion-or-lack-thereof</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2454496-dustin-crazy-little-brown-owl?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2454496-dustin-crazy-little-brown-owl?shelf=disturbing">disturbing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2454496-dustin-crazy-little-brown-owl?shelf=amazing">amazing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2454496-dustin-crazy-little-brown-owl?shelf=worth-reading-again-and-again">worth-reading-again-and-again</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3199916070" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7995863939487739909">A message of embracing change and a hope for the future.<br/><br/>Parable of the Talents is the second book in the Earthseed Series and sequel to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52397.Parable_of_the_Sower__Earthseed___1_' title='Parable of the Sower (Earthseed, #1) by Octavia E. Butler' rel='nofollow noopener'>Parable of the Sower</a>. Earthseed was meant to be a trilogy, but Octavia E. Butler died in 2006, before finishing Parable of the Trickster.<br/><br/>There have been so many fictional stories set in the near future - I often wonder how many it takes for us to wake up to the parallels, and why people are generally surprised by current events. The truth is stranger than fictio</span>
  <span id="freeText7995863939487739909" style="display:none">A message of embracing change and a hope for the future.<br /><br />Parable of the Talents is the second book in the Earthseed Series and sequel to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52397.Parable_of_the_Sower__Earthseed___1_" title="Parable of the Sower (Earthseed, #1) by Octavia E. Butler" rel="nofollow noopener">Parable of the Sower</a>. Earthseed was meant to be a trilogy, but Octavia E. Butler died in 2006, before finishing Parable of the Trickster.<br /><br />There have been so many fictional stories set in the near future - I often wonder how many it takes for us to wake up to the parallels, and why people are generally surprised by current events. The truth is stranger than fiction. <br /><br />Originally published in 1998, Parable of The Talents, like it's predecessor, is an eerily prophetic cautionary tale. For example, take these references of a post-millennial presidential candidate described in Parable of the Talents: <br /><br /><i>Jarret insists on being a throwback to some earlier, "simpler" time. Now does not suit him. Religious tolerance does not suit him. The current state of the country does not suit him. He wants to take us all back to some magical time when everyone believed in the same God, worshipped him in the same way, and understood that their safety in the universe depended on completing the same religious rituals and stomping anyone who was different. There was never such a time in this country. But these days when more than half the people in the country can't read at all, history is just one more vast unknown to them.<br />_______<br /><br />And "cultist" is a great catchall term for anyone who fits into no other large category, and yet doesn't quite match Jarret's version of Christianity. Jarret's people have been known to beat or drive out Unitarians, for goodness' sake. Jarret condemns the burnings, but does so in such a mild language that his people are free to hear what they want to hear. As for the beatings, the tarring and feathering, and the destruction of "heathen houses of devil-worship," he has a simple answer:<br />"Join us! Our doors are open to every nationality, every race! Leave your sinful past behind, and become one of us. Help us to make American great again."<br />_______<br /><br />We have, it seems, a few people who think Jarret may be just what the country needs--apart from his religious nonsense. The thing is, you can't separate Jarret from the "religious nonsense." You take Jarret and you get beatings, burnings, tarrings and featherings. They're a package. And there may be even nastier things in that package. Jarret's supporters are more than a little seduced by Jarret's talk of making America great again. He seems to be unhappy with certain other countries. We could wind up in a war. Nothing like a war to rally people around flag, country and great leader.<br />________ <br /><br />"Once he's made everyone who isn't like him sound evil, then he can blame them for problems he knows they didn't cause. That's easier than trying to fix the problems."<br />"My dad says . . ." The boy stopped and swallowed. "My dad said Jarret's and idiot."<br />"I agree with your dad."<br />________<br /><br />Yet Andrew Steele Jarret was able to scare, divide, and bully people, first into electing him President, then into letting him fix the country for them. He didn't get to do everything he wanted to do. He was capable of much greater fascism. So were his most avid followers.<br />________<br /><br />In less than a year, Jarret went from being our savior, almost the Second Coming in some people's minds, to being an incompetent son of a bitch who was wasting our substance on things that didn't matter. I don't mean that everyone changed their feelings toward him. Many people never did.<br /></i><br /><br />Favorite Passages from Parable of the Talents:<br /><i><br />FROM Memories of Other Worlds<br />BY TAYLOR FRANKLIN BANKOLE<br /><br />I have read that the period of upheaval that journalists have begun to refer to as "the Apocalypse" or more commonly, more bitterly, "the Pox" lasted from 2015 through 2030--a decade and a half of chaos. This is untrue. The Pox has been a much longer torment. It began well before 2015, perhaps before the turn of the millennium. It has not ended.<br />I have also read that the Pox was caused by accidentally coinciding climatic, economic, and sociological crises. It would be more honest to say that the Pox was caused by our own refusal to deal with obvious problems in those areas. We caused the problems then we sat and watched as they grew into crises. I have heard people deny this, but I was born in 1970. I have seen enough to know that it is true.<br />_______<br /><br />FROM Memories of Other Worlds<br /><br />I cannot know what the end will be of all of Olamina's dreaming, striving, and certainty. I cannot recall ever feeling as certain of anything as she seems to be of Earthseed, a belief system that she herself created--or, as she says, a network of truths that she has simply recognized. I was always a doubter when it came to religion. How irrational of me, then, to love a zealot. But then, both love and zealotry are irrational states of mind.<br />Olamina believes in a god that does not in the least love her. In fact, her god is a process or a combination of processes, not an entity. It is not consciously aware of her--or of anything. It is not conscious at all. "God is Change," she says and means it. Some of the faces of her god are biological evolution, chaos theory, relativity theory, the uncertainty principle, and, of course, the second law of thermodynamics. "God is Change, and, in the end, God prevails."<br />Yet Earthseed is not a fatalistic belief system. God can be directed, focused, speeded, slowed, shaped. All things change, but all things need not change in all ways. God is inexorable, yet malleable. Odd. Hardly religious at all. Even the Earthseed Destiny seems to have little to do with religion.<br />"We are Earthseed," Olamina says. "We are the children of God, as all fractions of the universe are the children of God. But more immediately we are the children of our particular Earth." And within those words likes the origin of the Destiny. The portion of humanity that is conscious, that knows it is Earthseed, and that accepts its Destiny is simply trying to leave the womb, the Earth, to be born as all young beings must do eventually.<br />Earthseed is Olamina's contribution to what she feels should be a species-wide effort to evade, or at least to lengthen the specialize-grow-die evolutionary cycle that humanity faces, that every species faces.<br />"We can be a long-term success and the parents, ourselves, of a vast array of new peoples, new species," she says, "or we can be just one more abortion. We can, we must, scatter the Earth's living essence--human, plant, and animal--to extrasolar worlds: 'The Destiny of Earthseed is to take root among the stars.'"<br />Grand words.<br />She hopes and dreams and writes and believes, and perhaps the world will let her live for a while, tolerating her as a harmless eccentric. I hope that it will. I fear that it may not.<br />________<br /><br />. . . Alaska, weary of regulations and restrictions from far away Washington, D.C., and even more weary of the hoard of hopeful paupers flooding in, declared itself an independent country. It seceded from the United States. First time since the Civil War that a state's done that. <br />_______<br /><br />Today we gave the Noyer children oak seedlings to plant in earth that has been mixed with the ashes of their parents. We do this in memory of our dead, present and absent. None of the ashes of my family are here, but five years ago when we decided to stay here, I planted trees in their memory.<br />_______<br /><br />A few miles to the north and south of us in the old national and state parks, the groves of ancient giants still stand. . . . .If people will let them alone, they will go on, childless, anachronistic, but still alive, still reaching futilely skyward. <br />________<br /><br />"Praying does work. Praying is a very effective way of talking to yourself, of talking yourself into thing, of focusing your attention on whatever it is you want to do. It can give you a feeling of control and help you to stretch yourself beyond what you thought were your limits."<br />_______<br /><br />They say we have no business wasting time or money in space when there are so many people suffering here on Earth, here in America. I wonder, though, where the money received in exchange for the installations has gone. I haven't noticed any new government education or jobs programs. There's been no government help for the homeless, the sick, the hungry. Squatter settlements are as big and as nasty as ever. As a country, we've given up our birthright for even less than bread and pottage. We've given it up for nothing - although I'm sure some people somewhere are richer now. <br />_______<br /><br />FROM Warrior BY MARCUS DURAN<br /><br />I've read history. Things weren't always this way. They don't have to go on being this way. What we have broken we can mend.<br />_______<br /><br />From EARTHSEED: THE BOOKS OF THE LIVING<br />To make peace with others,<br />Make peace with yourself:<br />Shape God<br />With generosity<br />And compassion.<br />Minimize harm.<br />Shield the weak.<br />Treasure the innocent.<br />Be true to the Destiny.<br />Forgive your enemies.<br />Forgive yourself.<br />_______<br /><br />From EARTHSEED: THE BOOKS OF THE LIVING<br /><br />Choose your leaders <br />with wisdom and forethought.<br />To be led by a coward<br />is to be controlled<br />by all that the coward fears.<br />To be led by a fool<br />is to be led by the opportunists<br />who control the fool.<br />To be led by a thief<br />is to offer up <br />your most precious treasures <br />to be stolen.<br />To be led by a liar <br />is to ask <br />to be told lies.<br />To be led by a tyrant<br />is to sell yourself <br />and those you love<br />into slavery.<br />_______<br /><br />From EARTHSEED: THE BOOKS OF THE LIVING<br /><br />Beware:<br />Ignorance protects itself.<br />Ignorance promotes suspicion.<br />Suspicion engenders fear.<br />Fear quails, irrational and blind,<br />Or fear looms, defiant and closed.<br />Blind, closed, suspicious, afraid,<br />Ignorance protects itself,<br />And protected ignorance grows. <br />________<br /><br />And, of course, breaking people is much easier than putting them together again. <br />________<br /><br />Quiet was good. Questioning was bad. Children should be seen and not heard. They should believe what their elders told them, and be content that it was all they needed to know. If there were any brutality in the way I was raised, that was it. Stupid faith was good. Thinking and questioning were bad. <br />________<br /><br />God is Change. I've taught that for six years. It's true, and I suppose it's paved the way for us now. Earthseed prepares you to live in the world that is and try to shape the world that you want. But none of it is really easy.<br />________<br /><br />From EARTHSEED: THE BOOKS OF THE LIVING<br /><br />The Destiny of Earthseed is to take root among the stars.<br />It is to live and to thrive on new earths.<br />It is to become new beings and to consider new questions.<br />It is to leap into the heavens again and again.<br />It is to explore the vastness of heaven.<br />It is to explore the vastness of ourselves. <br />_______<br /><br />"Kindness eases Change. Love quiets fear."<br />_______<br /><br />To shape God with Wisdom and forethought<br />To benefit your world, Your people, Your Life.<br />Consider consequences. Minimize harm.<br />Ask questions. Seek answers.<br />Learn. Teach.<br />_______<br /><br />". . . the world is full of needy people. They don't all need the same things, but they all need purpose. Even some of the ones with plenty of money need purpose."<br />_______<br /><br />And I'm finding more and more people who have the leisure now to worry about the nasty, downward slide the country's been on. In the 2020s, when these people were sick, starving, or trying to keep warm, they had no time or energy to look beyond their own desperate situations. Now, though, as they're more able to meet their own immediate needs, they begin to look around, feel dissatisfied with the slow pace of change . . . <br />_______<br /><br />From EARTHSEED: THE BOOKS OF THE LIVING<br /><br />To survive,<br />Let the past teach you--<br />Past customs, struggles, Leaders and thinkers.<br />Let these help you.<br />Let them inspire you, warn you, give you strength.<br />But beware: <br />God is Change. Past is past. <br />What was cannot come again.<br /><br />To survive,<br />Know the past.<br />Let it touch you.<br />Then let the past go.<br />_______<br /><br />"That's what Earthseed was about," I said. "I wanted us to understand what we could be, what we could do. I wanted to give us a focus, a goal, something big enough, complex enough, difficult enough, and in the end, radical enough to make us become more than we ever have been. We keep falling into the same ditches, you know? I mean, we learn more and more about the physical universe, more about our own bodies, more technology, but somehow, down through history, we go on building empires of one kind or another, then destroying them in one way or another. We go on having stupid wars that we justify and get passionate about, but in the end, all they do is kill huge numbers of people, maim others, impoverish still more, spread disease and hunger, and set the stage for the next war. And when we look at all of that in history, we just shrug our shoulders and say, well, that's the way things are. That's the way things have always been."<br />"It is," Len said<br />"It is," I repeated. "There seem to be solid biological reasons why we are the way we are. If there weren't, the cycles wouldn't keep replaying. The human species is a kind of animal, of course. But w can do something no other animal species has ever had the option to do. We can choose: We can go on building and destroying until we either destroy ourselves or destroy the ability of our world to sustain us. Or we can make something more for ourselves. We can grow up. We can leave the nest. We can fulfill the Destiny, make homes for ourselves among the stars, and become some combination of what we want to become and whatever our new environments challenge us to become. Our new worlds will remake us as we remake them. And some of the new people who emerge from all this will develop new ways to cope. They'll have to. That will break the old cycle, even if it's only to begin a new one, a different one.<br />"Earthseed is about preparing to fulfill the Destiny. It's about learning to live in partnership with one another in small communities, and at the same time, working out a sustainable partnership with our environment. It's about treating education and adaptability as the absolute essentials that they are. It's . . . " I glanced at Len, caught a little smile on her face, and wound down. "It's about a lot more than that," I said. "But those are the bones."<br />"Makes a strange sermon."<br />"I know."</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="7995863939487739909" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12298337380278309640">2.5/5<br/>Two major problems lead to this book getting the rating it did<br/>--The first one was completely the book's fault and that was extremely mediocre dialogue. Like its been a while since I've read dialogue this colorless.<br/>--The second was that this is the second book in a duology...and i didnt read the first book.   <span id="freeText12298337380278309640" style="display:none">2.5/5<br />Two major problems lead to this book getting the rating it did<br />--The first one was completely the book's fault and that was extremely mediocre dialogue. Like its been a while since I've read dialogue this colorless.<br />--The second was that this is the second book in a duology...and i didnt read the first book. Bare in mind that you absolutely don't need to read the first book to read this one. But since i review books more from a writers standpoint than that of enjoyment, i noticed a lot of bugs that came from me having no prior knowledge of character or world. <br /><br />That being said Octavia E Butler was able to create her own religion really well, and she did give a nice commentary on slave and rape culture. So the book wasn't all bad.</span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10502518945530134263">Maybe Butler was clairvoyant, but mostly she was a student of history who used her brilliant imagination to envision a chilling world in our near future. I appreciated her addition of POVs in this 2nd Earthseed book. The mother-daughter contrast was stark. And the violence was rough to digest. But Talents cemented Butler as a favorite of mine, if not THE favorite. <br/><br/>Full review on my book blog <a href='http://thebibliophage.com/book-review-parable-talents-octavia-butler-earthseed/' rel='nofollow noopener'>TheBibliophage.com</a>.</span>
  <span id="freeText10502518945530134263" style="display:none">Maybe Butler was clairvoyant, but mostly she was a student of history who used her brilliant imagination to envision a chilling world in our near future. I appreciated her addition of POVs in this 2nd Earthseed book. The mother-daughter contrast was stark. And the violence was rough to digest. But Talents cemented Butler as a favorite of mine, if not THE favorite. <br /><br />Full review on my book blog <a href="http://thebibliophage.com/book-review-parable-talents-octavia-butler-earthseed/" rel="nofollow noopener">TheBibliophage.com</a>.</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ZEtvyLJS0RO9ziZ1K52PG9sjlqLucleqtb+MapiWdQxr89/nGrhfUQRFDoOJAfWs/y6m6tmLeuHcBtupci6fdw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('AfEHNkJXGeMwBRMJlPE+wi7eVRP2PfZDx4WzF4c1RwYOSbcZ6r2XoYmOO/82bUR1CtNlW8HE2wiuPOTUbY2tfQ==')})
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_108428'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/108428.Are_Prisons_Obsolete_?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Are Prisons Obsolete?<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/5863103.Angela_Y_Davis?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Angela Y. Davis<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><\/span> 4.53 avg rating &mdash; 10,658 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2003\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer7953912167542036996\">With her characteristic brilliance, grace and radical audacity, Angela Y. Davis has put the case for the latest abolition movement in American life: the abolition of the prison. As she quite correctly notes, American life is replete with abolition mo<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7953912167542036996\" style=\"display:none\">With her characteristic brilliance, grace and radical audacity, Angela Y. Davis has put the case for the latest abolition movement in American life: the abolition of the prison. As she quite correctly notes, American life is replete with abolition movements, and when they were engaged in these struggles, their chances of success seemed almost unthinkable. For generations of Americans, the abolition of slavery was sheerest illusion. Similarly,the entrenched system of racial segregation seemed to last forever, and generations lived in the midst of the practice, with few predicting its passage from custom. The brutal, exploitative (dare one say lucrative?) convict-lease system that succeeded formal slavery reaped millions to southern jurisdictions (and untold miseries for tens of thousands of men, and women). Few predicted its passing from the American penal landscape. Davis expertly argues how social movements transformed these social, political and cultural institutions, and made such practices untenable.In Are Prisons Obsolete?, Professor Davis seeks to illustrate that the time for the prison is approaching an end. She argues forthrightly for &quot;decarceration&quot;, and argues for the transformation of the society as a whole.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"7953912167542036996\" href=\"#\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/29535.Octavia_E_Butler">About Octavia E. Butler</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/29535.Octavia_E_Butler"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1242244143p3/29535.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/29535.Octavia_E_Butler">Octavia E. Butler
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
9,572
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1659647422092265400">Octavia Estelle Butler was an American science fiction writer, one of the best-known among the few African-American women in the field. She won both Hugo and Nebula awards. In 1995, she became the first science fiction writer to receive the MacArthur Foundation "Genius" Grant.</span>

</div>
</div>

One of the world’s most respected authors of science fiction imagines an apocalyptic near-future Earth where a remarkable young woman discovers that her destiny calls her to try and change the world around her. Octavia E. Butler’s brilliant two-volume Earthseed saga offers a startling vision of an all-too-possible tomorrow, in which walls offer no protection from a civilization gone mad.  Parable of the Sower: In the aftermath of worldwide ecological and economic apocalypse, minister’s daughter Lauren Oya Olamina escapes the slaughter that claims the lives of her family and nearly every other member of their gated California community. Heading north with two young companions through an American wasteland, the courageous young woman faces dangers at every turn while spreading the word of a remarkable new religion that embraces survival and change.  Parable of the Talents: Called to the new, hard truth of Earthseed, the small community of the dispossessed that now surrounds Lauren Olamina looks to her—their leader—for guidance. But when the evil that has grown out of the ashes of human society destroys all she has built, the prophet is forced to choose between preserving her faith or her family.   The Earthseed novels cement Butler’s reputation as “one of the finest voices in fiction—period” (TheWashington Post Book World). Stunningly prescient and breathtakingly relevant to our times, this dark vision of a future America is a masterwork of powerful speculation that ushers us into a broken, dangerously divided world of
